<div id="review_1181857023" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1181857023" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2455537-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402467471p2/2455537.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1181857023?book_show_action=true">Jan 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/2455537-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1181857023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6523983435553073758">I knew I was gonna like this book a lot when I decided to read it, but I didn't know how much it was going to CONSUME MY LIFE for a few days (goodbye work ethic, but also why this is getting five stars rather than four). <br/><br/>The writing is absolutely exquisite. There were times when I put the book down and thought how much I loved the line I had just read. I had forgotten how much I miss this style of writing, that early 20th century British novelist writing that I read so much of in my adolescence </span>
  <span id="freeText6523983435553073758" style="display:none">I knew I was gonna like this book a lot when I decided to read it, but I didn't know how much it was going to CONSUME MY LIFE for a few days (goodbye work ethic, but also why this is getting five stars rather than four). <br /><br />The writing is absolutely exquisite. There were times when I put the book down and thought how much I loved the line I had just read. I had forgotten how much I miss this style of writing, that early 20th century British novelist writing that I read so much of in my adolescence and don't read as much now. I think that's why I had such a strong sense of familiarity with it. (I think the setting had a lot to do with it too: I mean, I've read <i>The Lute Player</i> and <i>The Eagle of the Ninth</i> kind of recently, but they were both historical novels. I haven't really read books set in Britain during the first half of the twentieth century <i>by</i> someone who lived during that time in a loooong while, maybe not since I was eighteen? And that was the kind of book I read predominantly in my adolescence so I think that's another element to it.) On the other hand, it is a challenging novel to read for a reason I adore in books: there is so much left unsaid, so much you have to gather from what the characters are dancing about saying but aren't saying outright. It requires close reading. <br /><br />The characters are all so wonderfully complex, even if you only get glimpses of a couple of them. How much of the story is it that these people are really what they are like, and how much of them is because of Laurie projecting his own ideals onto them? <br /><br />It's just as much a war novel and its effects on the men involved in it as it is about the relationships (romantic and not) between these men. I want to talk more about this book but that's getting into spoiler territory. But this is an intense and sophisticated novel, eviscerating in its analysis of the characters yet not without its moments of compassion and beauty. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6523983435553073758" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3940665" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Falkor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Falkor" href="/user/show/241581-falkor">Falkor</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of romance, esp. male/male romance; people interested in pre-Stonewall gay life</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241581-falkor?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/241581-falkor?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3940665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9892955935561412221">Laurie O'Dell is seriously wounded during the early days of World War II. While recuperating in an English hospital, he is entangled in a love triangle. On one side is Andrew, a pious, naive young conscientious objector who works in the hospital as an orderly; on the other is Ralph, an old school friend who, despite having many affairs with men and women while traveling the world in the navy, still harbors strong feelings for Laurie. Laurie is paralyzed with indecision. He can have a passionate </span>
  <span id="freeText9892955935561412221" style="display:none">Laurie O'Dell is seriously wounded during the early days of World War II. While recuperating in an English hospital, he is entangled in a love triangle. On one side is Andrew, a pious, naive young conscientious objector who works in the hospital as an orderly; on the other is Ralph, an old school friend who, despite having many affairs with men and women while traveling the world in the navy, still harbors strong feelings for Laurie. Laurie is paralyzed with indecision. He can have a passionate and fulfilling romantic relationship with Ralph, risking total social ostracism and prison, if the affair is discovered. Or he can continue on the safe but repressive course of hiding his sexuality and silently loving Andrew, who does not know Laurie is gay and whose own sexual orientation is ambiguous. But if Laurie does not decide, he will lose both of the men he loves.<br /><br /><i>The Charioteer</i> is, for a book published in the mainstream press in the 1950s, remarkably frank about the sexuality of its characters. It details the secrecy and fear that gay men lived with in a time when being openly homosexual was unthinkable, and honest about the problems of the gay social scene: blackmail, alcoholism, depression and difficulty in forming lasting relationships. But the center of the book is the enduring love that the main characters have for each other despite all obstacles: Ralph's tender devotion to Laurie, even after not seeing him for seven years; the chaste but intense emotional connection Andrew has with Laurie; and the love Laurie feels for each of them, which is painful but also a source of joy and liberation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9892955935561412221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">Nabokov fans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Aitziber by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/10304884-fatima">Fatima</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26779314-aitziber?shelf=sexuality">sexuality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26779314-aitziber?shelf=language-english">language-english</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer796288876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2001669944671075778">I'm not sure that my review can ever do <i>The Charioteer</i> justice. One look at other people's reviews and it'll be immediately apparent that Renault has touched her readers deeply, and I'm no less than everyone else. <br/><br/><i>The Charioteer</i> is a novel to be savored. It works on more than one level, and in fact it's one of those books that can be re-read to discover what you missed the first, second, third time around. When you have finally extracted all the meanings you are able to, it is time to read <i>The P</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2001669944671075778" style="display:none">I'm not sure that my review can ever do <i>The Charioteer</i> justice. One look at other people's reviews and it'll be immediately apparent that Renault has touched her readers deeply, and I'm no less than everyone else. <br /><br /><i>The Charioteer</i> is a novel to be savored. It works on more than one level, and in fact it's one of those books that can be re-read to discover what you missed the first, second, third time around. When you have finally extracted all the meanings you are able to, it is time to read <i>The Phaedrus</i> and understand all the references that Renault makes to it.<br /><br />I was deeply moved by this novel. I feel that I barely scratched at the surface of it, and yet I was already so ensnared by it. I love a book that tricks you, and while I wouldn't say that Renault is a trickster, she definitely writes so that Laurie's confusion is extended to the reader, and so that what we think we know, or want, is not true at all. And if a book can do that to us, so shall we ask ourselves, what if life has also done?</span>
  <a data-text-id="2001669944671075778" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3282428868" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3282428868" />
    <a title="Lea" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24500926-lea"><img alt="Lea" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589618620p2/24500926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3282428868?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lea" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lea" href="/user/show/24500926-lea">Lea</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781844089505" href="/book/show/18758839-the-charioteer">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24500926-lea?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24500926-lea?shelf=war-military">war-military</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24500926-lea?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24500926-lea?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24500926-lea?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3282428868" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1234686730308577828">So far my favourite Mary Renault novel (and I really liked the Persian Boy a lot, too!). There's just something about the 'contemporary' setting - contemporary for her time - that works even better for me then the historic settings. You can really tell she knew what she was writing about, working as a nurse post world war II herself. I really felt like getting a glimpse into lives I'd otherwise never have read about. <br/><br/>On the surface, this could be seen as a classical love triangle: Laurie Odell, </span>
  <span id="freeText1234686730308577828" style="display:none">So far my favourite Mary Renault novel (and I really liked the Persian Boy a lot, too!). There's just something about the 'contemporary' setting - contemporary for her time - that works even better for me then the historic settings. You can really tell she knew what she was writing about, working as a nurse post world war II herself. I really felt like getting a glimpse into lives I'd otherwise never have read about. <br /><br />On the surface, this could be seen as a classical love triangle: Laurie Odell, a young corporal wounded at Dunkirk, is recovering at a veterans' hospital where he meets Andrew, a conchie (conscientious objector to the war) and quaker. They form a friendship. It is clear that both of them desire it to be more than that, but it's difficult for a number of societal and personal reasons, and their relationship doesn't get further than one chaste kiss. During all of this, Laurie meets Ralph, someone he already had a bond with at school, and they get close too. I honestly didn't know where this novel was heading, and at the end of this the book really become a page-turner. I was surprised by how dramatic it became, as it moved quite slowly in the beginning - but I really liked the varying of pacing. <br /><br />This book is superbly written. I marveled at the author's "show don't tell" mastering, although at times I had difficulty following the conversations when there seemed to be much more said between the lines, some of which I didn't get because of the 40s language. To say this is "merely" a love story wouldn't do it justice. It's also about the war, friendship, societal norms, and most of all, it's a study of characters. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1234686730308577828" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1206926239" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1206926239" />
    <a title="Stephen Groenewegen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6716718-stephen-groenewegen"><img alt="Stephen Groenewegen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1206926239?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen Groenewegen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen Groenewegen" href="/user/show/6716718-stephen-groenewegen">Stephen Groenewegen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1206926239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18433345888494605365">Novels, like films and other art forms, date. The best works of art tap into something universal, and timeless, and can be read and enjoyed across the decades. Some novels are so full of contemporary mood and feeling, they quickly become period pieces and snapshots of a particular time and place.<br/><br/><i>The Charioteer</i> is about a love triangle between three men in England during the Second World War. It was written in the 1950s and covers gay love as you’d expect a British novel of the 1950s to: discreet</span>
  <span id="freeText18433345888494605365" style="display:none">Novels, like films and other art forms, date. The best works of art tap into something universal, and timeless, and can be read and enjoyed across the decades. Some novels are so full of contemporary mood and feeling, they quickly become period pieces and snapshots of a particular time and place.<br /><br /><i>The Charioteer</i> is about a love triangle between three men in England during the Second World War. It was written in the 1950s and covers gay love as you’d expect a British novel of the 1950s to: discreetly. At the time I’m sure it was shocking; today, the coding in some of the language verges on the impenetrable.<br /><br />Mary Renault, a lesbian who wrote a number of novels with gay themes, was also a classicist and her later novels were set in Ancient Greece. The title of the book refers to an allegory of Plato’s about a charioteer (human soul) steering a course with one horse driven by noble aims, the other by more earthly desires.<br /><br />Our charioteer is Laurie ‘Spud’ Odell, introduced first as a young boy on the night that his father leaves home, never to return. Then we take up his story as a teenager during a pivotal moment at his posh public school in England. An older boy - the prefect Ralph Lanyon - is departing school in mysterious circumstances and has a final, loaded conversation with Odell. Renault is so careful with her language here, it took me a while to work out the exact nature of the scandalous circumstances leading to Lanyon’s departure.<br /><br />The bulk of the book takes place a few years later in a military hospital in England after 1940. Odell is recovering from a leg injury he sustained at Dunkirk. When a group of Quaker conscientious objectors are brought in to assist the nurses, many of the soldiers and hospital staff are contemptuous of the ‘conchie’ cowards. But Odell strikes up a friendship with one of them, an attractive younger man named Andrew. He and Andrew share some intimate late night talks and afternoon walks, but Odell sees him as uncorrupted and pure and decides not to broach his feelings for him.<br /><br />Odell’s life becomes more complicated when a doctor, recognising a fellow gay man, invites him to a clandestine party. Odell meets a number of gay men and is reunited with Lanyon, now a naval officer. Odell idolised Lanyon at school, and it seems Lanyon also desires Odell. But what of Odell’s feelings for Andrew?<br /><br />The love triangle is heartfelt and I became emotionally involved in the story and Odell’s romantic dilemma. Does he honour his Platonic feelings for Andrew or submit to his earthly, reciprocated desire for Lanyon? <br /><br />Renault offers a rich gallery of supporting characters - including Odell’s mum, who marries a man her son despises; and Reg, a straight chum of Odell’s in the hospital, whom he can’t bring into his confidence. It’s refreshing to read a book about this period based not at the front but in a hospital so close to home (I was reminded of Pat Barker’s <i>Regeneration</i> trilogy about the First World War).<br /><br />The language is rich, poetic - and frequently obscure (but sure to become clearer on subsequent readings). A lot is left unsaid, which can be maddening to a modern audience, accustomed to the ‘love that dare not speak its name’ being more vocal. But if you are not put off by the language, there are tender riches to be mined in the story of <i>The Charioteer</i>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18433345888494605365" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3531664519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3531664519" />
    <a title="Charles Edwards-Freshwater" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75779839-charles-edwards-freshwater"><img alt="Charles Edwards-Freshwater" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575812240p2/75779839.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3531664519?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charles Edwards-Freshwater" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charles Edwards-Freshwater" href="/user/show/75779839-charles-edwards-freshwater">Charles Edwards-Freshwater</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3531664519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10815347246210627159">First read of September and it was a good one!<br/><br/>Set among war torn Britain during the blitz, the story centers around a gay soldier (Laurie, or Spud/Spuddy to those who know him) and his struggle with his affections for a sweet, mild conchie man who works at the hospital and his ex school fellow whom he runs into while recovering from a nasty leg injury incurred at Dunkirk.<br/><br/>There's a lot to absolutely love here - beautifully woven relationships, gorgeous prose, characters with a great psychological</span>
  <span id="freeText10815347246210627159" style="display:none">First read of September and it was a good one!<br /><br />Set among war torn Britain during the blitz, the story centers around a gay soldier (Laurie, or Spud/Spuddy to those who know him) and his struggle with his affections for a sweet, mild conchie man who works at the hospital and his ex school fellow whom he runs into while recovering from a nasty leg injury incurred at Dunkirk.<br /><br />There's a lot to absolutely love here - beautifully woven relationships, gorgeous prose, characters with a great psychological depth to them - the whole scenario is very real. Some reviewers have stated that the supporting cast of gay men are all bitchy queens and this is offensive, but to me I think that's not necessarily the case - anyone will know that people with this sort of personality do exist, but even so, characters such as Bunny had a certain depth to them - a sad inability to keep up with the Plato-spewing glorified main gay characters which manifested in petty actions. It all seemed very authentic.<br /><br />Renault also has a fabulous way of writing a paragraph full of meaning that you really need to knuckle down with and reread a couple of times to fully understand. In a more ordinary story this would be frustrating, but here it echoes the secrecy of the gay characters and their need to remain hidden. Things are kept on the down low even to the reader, and in a way where you have to strip back the layers to really understand what's going on. Masterful stuff. There is an argument that this makes the story feel antiseptic and without sexual desire, but I disagree here too - it's a sort of closed desire, a pure kind of attraction - it's subtle but powerful.<br /><br />My only real problem with this novel is that it didn't end how I wanted it to - although the ending is definitely on the more positive side. I think I too was suckered in by the other candidate of the love triangle and felt that Laurie deserved better, but I see how this ending does make more sense in the grand scheme of things and, perhaps, is more romantic in some ways.<br /><br />A landmark piece of gay literature that deserves more attention.<br /><br />4.5 - 5 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="10815347246210627159" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="clear"></div>
</div>

  </div>
    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>



  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1358684774" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1358684774" />
    <a title="R.a." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2456272-r-a"><img alt="R.a." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481060031p2/2456272.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1358684774?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="R.a." class="user" itemprop="url" name="R.a." href="/user/show/2456272-r-a">R.a.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2456272-r-a?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2456272-r-a?shelf=fic-gen">fic-gen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2456272-r-a?shelf=fic-historical">fic-historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2456272-r-a?shelf=fic-romance-love">fic-romance-love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1358684774" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14944156277426541481">4.75 stars<br/><br/><blockquote>Can life be a blessing,<br/>Or worth the possessing,<br/>Can life be a blessing, if love were away?<br/>Ah, no! though our love all night keep us waking,<br/>And though he torment us with cares all the day,<br/>Yet he sweetens, he sweetens our pains in the taking;<br/>There’s an hour at the last, there’s an hour to repay.*</blockquote><br/>The just, the beautiful, and the good.<br/>What “is” and what “is becoming.”<br/><br/>Love. Identity.<br/><br/>How a soul makes the “right” choice.<br/>In life.<br/>In love.<br/><br/>This is <b>all</b>.<br/><br/>This is all for Laurence, “Laurie,” Odell, 23,</span>
  <span id="freeText14944156277426541481" style="display:none">4.75 stars<br /><br /><blockquote>Can life be a blessing,<br />Or worth the possessing,<br />Can life be a blessing, if love were away?<br />Ah, no! though our love all night keep us waking,<br />And though he torment us with cares all the day,<br />Yet he sweetens, he sweetens our pains in the taking;<br />There’s an hour at the last, there’s an hour to repay.*</blockquote><br />The just, the beautiful, and the good.<br />What “is” and what “is becoming.”<br /><br />Love. Identity.<br /><br />How a soul makes the “right” choice.<br />In life.<br />In love.<br /><br />This is <b>all</b>.<br /><br />This is all for Laurence, “Laurie,” Odell, 23, also known as “Spud,” or "Spuddy." He is still young, though wounded from action in the war, (World War II), and undergoing several operations on his leg, with treatments thereafter. And throughout his convalescence and wanderings, the war continues. Yet, at this time, in this place, for him, those two questions become all.<br /><br />And so, here is the heavy underpinning to Mary Renault’s superb “romance” novel, <i>The Charioteer</i>.<br /><br />On the surface, <i>The Charioteer</i> progresses with the impressions of a confused, young man as he navigates his way through a previous “crush,” a current “love,” the lurid decadence of a gay “party,” and the questions attendant to his own identity. The <i>New York Times</i> comment stating that “Miss Renault . . . introduces us into a world of emotions so delicate and private that the reader often feels like an intruder” becomes very apt. Renault's narrations and descriptions, with a “lyrical style,” emplace the reader seemingly with an eye view from her protagonist, Laurie: <br /><blockquote>. . . know for the first time the burden, prison, and mystery of his own uniqueness; he had not chosen this music he moved to, it had chosen him; His mind swept the room and a memory . . . transforming itself into anticipation, gave a long sigh of protest and whispered, ‘Never again.’</blockquote> Yes, more than many, many writers, Renault, so seemingly <b>un</b>influenced by the “clipped,” fragmented Modernist style, pens <b>a novel</b>. At face value, a reader, pending sensibility, may be relieved, (or disappointed), at the extreme with which she clips or cuts the sexually physical scenes. Indeed, with one, (and, there are but a few), the reader may have to re-read the paragraph to verify that indeed such an event <i>did</i> occur. And, I readily cannot think of another novel that treats this subject, (love), with such consideration, such weight, and / or such probity.<br /><br />And yet, as a prose narrative, some readers will experience obstacles or “blocks.” The first chapter, though establishing his relationship with both his mother and father rings heavily Freudian; and, some may feel that this perpetuates a “gay” stereotype. The reader, at times, may feel frustration with Laurie, himself. His “blindness” and, his insistence to “project,” due to <i>The Phaedrus</i> can frustrate. And, the “party” as well as some characters there are so “lost,” that the reader may recoil from some of them. Yet, if the reader continues, s/he will find Laurie’s justified reasonings for each decision he makes.<br /><br />With both an implicit and explicit sense of Plato’s <i>The Phaedrus</i> dialectic in mind—to include the attending charioteer myth, the reader consequently may see and feel a much “deeper” experience with this novel. For Laurie is no longer simply a gay or bisexual young man finding himself, but a “soul” searching for the “right,” “like-minded” lover / companion. Indeed, the “gay” element almost becomes incidental, in this sense, since the same paradigm applies to heterosexual “love,” per the allegory and dialogue. And, Renault provides mirroring heterosexual relationships throughout: Reg’s difficulty with Madge, Laurie himself, with Nurse Adrian, and Laurie’s Mother, with her engagement to Mr. Straike.<br /><br />Although a reader then may see the characters and plot simply as a “playing out” of Plato’s dialogue, s/he subsequently will miss the full impact of this novel. Its scope extends further, “filling out” the classical allegory with real people, in a real time, in a real place, and in real situations. And, as in real life, the multiple layers of real people often stray from the varied abstracted qualities of those Ancient Greek gods and their chariot processions.<br /><br />Still . . . A reader cannot help but wonder:<br /><br />Which god does Laurie’s chariot follow?<br />Then, which god does Andrew’s chariot follow?<br />Ralph?<br />Nurse Adrian?<br /><br />Of course, the reader, then, is able to easily turn the question:<br /><br />Which god does <b>my</b> chariot follow?<br /><br />Lastly, with a remarkable plot progression, there emerges a joyous aspect in <i>The Charioteer</i>. Yet, this “joy” comes not without cost. Like Shakespeare’s <i>Measure for Measure</i>, a play that heavily explores justice and “right” action, Renault presents the problematic: "life was so divided . . . "<br /><br />Such a revelation about Laurie with that striking realization!<br /><br />. . .<br /><br />And so, Renault’s <i>The Charioteer</i> ultimately becomes a novel <i>for all</i> since . . .<br /><blockquote>In every possessing<br />The ravishing blessing,<br />In every possessing the fruit of our pain,<br />Poor lovers forget long ages of anguish,<br />Whate’er they have suffered and done to obtain;<br />‘Tis a pleasure, a pleasure to sigh and to languish,<br />When we hope, when we hope to be happy again.</blockquote> *(Stanzas 1 and 2 respectively, Song from <i>Troilus and Cressida</i>, John Dryden).<br /><br />Yes, this surprisingly full, sometimes profound, and <b>great novel</b> is highly recommended.<br /><br /><br /><br />(Coming to this novel from a previous reading of Renault's <i>The Mask of Apollo</i>, I now wonder if her historical novels of Ancient Greece will be at <i>this</i> level of exploration and "weight." I shall soon discover!)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14944156277426541481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1594754767" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1594754767" />
    <a title="Gin Jenny (Reading the End)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1908768-gin-jenny-reading-the-end"><img alt="Gin Jenny (Reading the End)" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1594754767?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gin Jenny (Reading the End)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gin Jenny (Reading the End)" href="/user/show/1908768-gin-jenny-reading-the-end">Gin Jenny (Reading the End)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1908768-gin-jenny-reading-the-end?shelf=descent-into-madness">descent-into-madness</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1594754767" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14636788421704521998">Ah, yes, The Charioteer. By the matchless Mary Renault, my love for whom cannot be expressed in strong enough terms, the author of Fire from Heaven and The Persian Boy, which I read as a kid and have never stopped loving. The Charioteer is one of her earlier novels, set more in modern times (World War II), at an army hospital as it happens.<br/><br/>Basically the main character, Laurie (called Spud because his last name’s Odell, bless him) is wounded at Dunkirk and falls madly in love with a conscientious</span>
  <span id="freeText14636788421704521998" style="display:none">Ah, yes, The Charioteer. By the matchless Mary Renault, my love for whom cannot be expressed in strong enough terms, the author of Fire from Heaven and The Persian Boy, which I read as a kid and have never stopped loving. The Charioteer is one of her earlier novels, set more in modern times (World War II), at an army hospital as it happens.<br /><br />Basically the main character, Laurie (called Spud because his last name’s Odell, bless him) is wounded at Dunkirk and falls madly in love with a conscientious objector who is an orderly at his army hospital. And their chaste romance continues apace, because Laurie nobly fears that he will ruin everything for innocent Andrew if he tells him about homosexuality. I am not a big fan of Andrew’s, to be honest, because he gets all noble and offended about everything, which makes me tired, and plus it crushes me when Laurie’s all tense and snappy due to unrequited love. So meanwhile he is reunited with this guy he admired when they were in school together, before the guy got expelled for being a big gay, and they get along gorgeously and Ralph is rather sweetly gallant. P.S. I like Ralph better than Andrew, and if I were Laurie, I’d be like, Huh, now with Ralph I have a future that contains good conversations, good sex, and no hiding shit, whereas with Andrew it’s just the good conversations and endless mental torture, and the decision would be easy, but Laurie spends a lot of time agonizing over it.<br /><br />I can’t explain what makes this book so appealing to me. One thing is that they really do have good conversations. Mary Renault writes these beautiful dialogue sequences that are just impossibly eloquent with the things they’re saying and the things they’re not saying. I go green with envy reading it because I will never, ever be able to pack that much meaning and intensity into a line of dialogue, ever. And overall, it’s just such an understated and melancholy book, and I really do like Ralph an awful lot. He’s such a dear and he loves Laurie so much.<br /><br />I will add this caveat: There’s a fair bit of unpleasantness with the more effeminate gay characters. They all have idiotic names like Bim and Toto and Bunny, and they are all gossipy bitchy people trying to screw up everyone else’s lives by telling lies and reading diaries and making half-assed manipulative suicide attempts. Not very nice in Mary Renault and not incredibly defensible even though she was writing about people that actually existed in a certain environment to which she had been recently, and to her detriment, exposed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14636788421704521998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_544697917" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/544697917" />
    <a title="Alicja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4745220-alicja"><img alt="Alicja" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411681297p2/4745220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/544697917?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alicja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alicja" href="/user/show/4745220-alicja">Alicja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=historical-classic">historical-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=lgbt-gay">lgbt-gay</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=early-20th-century">early-20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=lgbt-fiction">lgbt-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=bookclub">bookclub</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=dream-library">dream-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4745220-alicja?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer544697917" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10782675265198434637">rating: 5/5<br/><br/>Laurie Odell, injured during World War II, is transferred to a veterans hospital. There he meets and falls in love with a Quaker Hospital attendant, Andrew Raynes, an innocent young man who hasn’t figured himself out yet. To complicate matters fate brings back into his life an old school friend, Ralph Lanyon, who is familiar with the pitfalls of being gay during that era and also develops feelings for him. Laurie, healing from his injury and at the crossroads of his life, must now mak</span>
  <span id="freeText10782675265198434637" style="display:none">rating: 5/5<br /><br />Laurie Odell, injured during World War II, is transferred to a veterans hospital. There he meets and falls in love with a Quaker Hospital attendant, Andrew Raynes, an innocent young man who hasn’t figured himself out yet. To complicate matters fate brings back into his life an old school friend, Ralph Lanyon, who is familiar with the pitfalls of being gay during that era and also develops feelings for him. Laurie, healing from his injury and at the crossroads of his life, must now make choices that will have a profound impact on his future.<br /><br />Woven within this modern love story are references to Greek mythology and Plato’s Phaedrus showing that the human experience, especially the experience of love, transcends time and culture. Renault uses her lyrical style of writing to pack symbolism, hidden meanings, and innuendo into each page of the text. She reveals the hidden world of homosexual relationships during World War II filled with interpersonal drama. <br /><br />It is also a novel of self-discovery and finding ones place in the world, especially when the discovery leads to a moral dilemma and criminality (as it was at the time). The three main characters are at very different points in their lives at the start of the novel. Laurie is at a point right between discovery of why he feels different and acceptance of himself. Ralph is comfortable in, knowledgeable about, and has experience with different facets of homosexual relationships and community. Andrew is completely blind about his inclinations and is just beginning to realize he is different. Not only is Renault able to show a whole spectrum of experience through these three characters but the story takes on a life of its own as their three lives collide and intertwine. <br /><br />It is an amazing novel, although after reading the magnificence that are some of her other works, this one doesn’t seem as refined as her later novels. However, as I said, still completely and utterly brilliant and so far above most others.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10782675265198434637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lZU/+PPuuJN8L4VP+KGG1WetjYCniXDbGGajQcwk13crdxkFdDz82bPtBQSEPWYNhIgztb4DXsPAZeSsWwJT4w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ze1oKEbUQZA9+Uoynp5uYzcfJK17Hmnc7jTniv3rhWxzD07VwQYF2vI7ynniAo671DqamGKUR8Q2N6Bnas0B+A==')})
  }

Powerful and artful, and ten years in the writing, it is a masterwork from Jamie O’Neill.Jim Mack is a naïve young scholar and the son of a foolish, aspiring shopkeeper. Doyler Doyle is the rough-diamond son—revolutionary and blasphemous—of Mr. Mack’s old army pal. Out at the Forty Foot, that great jut of rock where gentlemen bathe in the nude, the two boys make a pact: Doyler will teach Jim to swim, and in a year, on Easter of 1916, they will swim to the distant beacon of Muglins Rock and claim that island for themselves. But a chance meeting by the river soon becomes an intense, exhilarating, and all-consuming affair. After their camp duties are fulfilled, the pair spend a dreamlike few weeks camping in the countryside, bonding over an illicit copy of James Baldwin’s Giovanni’s Room. Inhabiting a beautiful natural world removed from society and its constraints, Ludwik and Janusz fall deeply in love. But in their repressive communist and Catholic society, the passion they share is utterly unthinkable.\n\nOnce they return to Warsaw, the charismatic Janusz quickly rises in the political ranks of the party and is rewarded with a highly-coveted position in the ministry. Ludwik is drawn toward impulsive acts of protest, unable to ignore rising food prices and the stark economic disparity around them. Their secret love and personal and political differences slowly begin to tear them apart as both men struggle to survive in a regime on the brink of collapse.\n\nShifting from the intoxication of first love to the quiet melancholy of growing up and growing apart, Swimming in the Dark is a potent blend of romance, post-war politics, intrigue, and history. Lyrical and sensual, immersive and intense, Tomasz Jedrowski has crafted an indelible and thought-provoking literary debut that explores freedom and love in all its incarnations.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/38185.Mary_Renault">About Mary Renault</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/38185.Mary_Renault"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1378247630p3/38185.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/38185.Mary_Renault">Mary Renault
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,020
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10657480585552999878">Mary Renault was an English writer best known for her historical novels set in Ancient Greece. In addition to vivid fictional portrayals of Theseus, Socrates, Plato and Alexander the Great, she wrote a non-fiction biography of Alexander.<br/><br/>Her historical novels are all set in ancient Greece. They include a pair of novels about the mythological hero Theseus and a trilogy about the career of Alexander </span>
  <span id="freeText10657480585552999878" style="display:none">Mary Renault was an English writer best known for her historical novels set in Ancient Greece. In addition to vivid fictional portrayals of Theseus, Socrates, Plato and Alexander the Great, she wrote a non-fiction biography of Alexander.<br /><br />Her historical novels are all set in ancient Greece. They include a pair of novels about the mythological hero Theseus and a trilogy about the career of Alexander the Great. In a sense, The Charioteer (1953), the story of two young gay servicemen in the 1940s who try to model their relationship on the ideals expressed in Plato's Phaedrus and Symposium, is a warm-up for Renault's historical novels. By turning away from the 20th century and focusing on stories about male lovers in the warrior societies of ancient Greece, Renault no longer had to deal with homosexuality and anti-gay prejudice as social "problems". Instead she was free to focus on larger ethical and philosophical concerns, while examining the nature of love and leadership. The Charioteer could not be published in the U.S. until 1959, after the success of The Last of the Wine proved that American readers and critics would accept a serious gay love story.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10657480585552999878" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

    n = load.length,

        first.parentNode.insertBefore(script, first);

    w.addEventListener('load', go, false);
